<div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F762%2F1%2F32\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/762/1/32</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n                      <P>We present a candidate for the most distant galaxy known to date with a photometric redshift of <I>z</I> = 10.7<SUP>+0.6</SUP>             <SUB>\u20130.4</SUB> (95% confidence limits; with <I>z</I> &lt; 9.5 galaxies of known types ruled out at 7.2\u03c3). This <I>J</I>-dropout Lyman break galaxy, named MACS0647-JD, was discovered as part of the Cluster Lensing and Supernova survey with <I>Hubble</I> (CLASH). We observe three magnified images of this galaxy due to strong gravitational lensing by the galaxy cluster MACSJ0647.7+7015 at <I>z</I> = 0.591. The images are magnified by factors of ~80, 7, and 2, with the brighter two observed at ~26th magnitude AB (~0.15 \u03bcJy) in the WFC3/IR F160W filter (~1.4-1.7 \u03bcm) where they are detected at <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />12\u03c3. All three images are also confidently detected at <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />6\u03c3 in F140W (~1.2-1.6 \u03bcm), dropping out of detection from 15 lower wavelength <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> filters (~0.2-1.4 \u03bcm), and lacking bright detections in <I>Spitzer</I>/IRAC 3.6 \u03bcm and 4.5 \u03bcm imaging (~3.2-5.0 \u03bcm). We rule out a broad range of possible lower redshift interlopers, including some previously published as high-redshift candidates. Our high-redshift conclusion is more conservative than if we had neglected a Bayesian photometric redshift prior. Given CLASH observations of 17 high-mass clusters to date, our discoveries of MACS0647-JD at <I>z</I> ~ 10.8 and MACS1149-JD at <I>z</I> ~ 9.6 are consistent with a lensed luminosity function extrapolated from lower redshifts. This would suggest that low-luminosity galaxies could have reionized the universe. However, given the significant uncertainties based on only two galaxies, we cannot yet rule out the sharp drop-off in number counts at <I>z</I> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10 suggested by field searches.</P>       </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/762/1/32&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/762/1/32&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2013ApJ...762...32C\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2013ApJ...762...32C&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2013ApJ...762...32C\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s1\" name=\"apj451260s1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Current models of structure formation suggest that the first galaxies formed at <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10 when the universe was <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 470 Myr old (Wise &amp; Abel <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r168\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r168\">2007</a>; Wise et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r169\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r169\">2008</a>; Greif et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r72\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r72\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r71\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r71\">2010</a>; and for recent reviews, see Bromm &amp; Yoshida <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r27\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r27\">2011</a> and Dunlop <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r52\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r52\">2012</a>). Observations may be closing in on these first galaxies with one <em>z</em> ~ 10 candidate detected in the Ultra Deep Field (UDFj-39546284; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>) and another strongly lensed by a galaxy cluster (MACS1149-JD; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>).</p><p>Intriguingly, the number density of <em>z</em> ~ 10 galaxies detected in unlensed fields is several times lower than predicted based on extrapolations from lower redshifts, assuming a luminosity function with one or more parameters evolving linearly with redshift (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r15\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r15\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>). This suggests that the star formation rate density (SFRD) built up more rapidly from <em>z</em> ~ 10 to 8 than it did later between <em>z</em> ~ 8 and 2. This is consistent with some theoretical predictions (Trenti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r163\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r163\">2010</a>; Lacey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r103\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r103\">2011</a>). However, Robertson &amp; Ellis (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r138\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r138\">2012</a>) suggest such a sharp drop-off would be in tension with <em>z</em> &lt; 4 gamma-ray burst rates as correlated with SFRD and extrapolated to higher redshifts. Direct detections and confirmations of <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10 galaxies are required to more precisely constrain the SFRD at that epoch.</p><p>The observed luminosity functions at <em>z</em> ~ 7 and 8 feature steep faint end slopes of \u03b1 ~ \u22122 (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r17\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r17\">2011b</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>), steeper than at lower redshifts, a trend consistent with model predictions (Trenti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r163\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r163\">2010</a>; Jaacks et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r82\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r82\">2012</a>). If these luminosity functions can be extrapolated to <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10, then low-luminosity galaxies (<em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> fainter than \u221216 AB) could have reionized the universe (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r18\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r18\">2012a</a>; Kuhlen &amp; Faucher-Gigu\u00e8re <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r100\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r100\">2012</a>), assuming a sufficient fraction of their UV photons could escape their host galaxies to the surrounding medium (see also Conroy &amp; Kratter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r43\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r43\">2012</a>). Otherwise, a more exotic source of reionizing energy may have been required, such as self-annihilating dark matter (Iocco <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r81\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r81\">2010</a>; Natarajan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r118\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r118\">2012</a>).</p><p>Reionization was likely well underway by <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10 but with over half the universe still neutral (Robertson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r139\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r139\">2010</a>; Pandolfi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r125\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r125\">2011</a>; Mitra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r114\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r114\">2012</a>). Improving our understanding of the early universe and this phase change is one of the pressing goals of modern cosmology.</p><p>Observations with the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3; Kimble et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r90\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r90\">2008</a>) installed on the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) have significantly advanced our understanding of the <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 7 universe, over 13 billion years in the past. The UDF and surrounding deep fields have yielded over 100 robust <em>z</em> &gt; 7 candidates as faint as 29th magnitude AB (Bunker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r30\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r30\">2010</a>; Labb\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r102\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r102\">2010</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r17\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r17\">2011b</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>). Analyses of wider space-based surveys such as CANDELS (Grogin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r73\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r73\">2011</a>; Koekemoer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r98\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r98\">2011</a>), BoRG (Trenti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r161\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r161\">2012</a>), and HIPPIES (Yan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r172\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r172\">2011</a>) have helped fill out the brighter end of the luminosity function (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r123\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r123\">2012b</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>; Yan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r172\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r172\">2011</a>).</p><p>Of the handful of <em>z</em> &gt; 7 galaxies spectroscopically confirmed to date, most have been discovered in even wider near-infrared surveys carried out with the ground-based telescopes Subaru (Shibuya et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r153\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r153\">2012</a>; Ono et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r124\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r124\">2012</a>), Very Large Telescope (VLT; Vanzella et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r166\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r166\">2011</a>), and UKIRT (Mortlock et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r116\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r116\">2011</a>). Surveys with the VISTA telescope are also beginning to yield high-redshift candidates (Bowler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r20\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r20\">2012</a>).</p><p>Complementary to these searches of \"blank\" fields are searches behind strongly lensing galaxy clusters (Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r93\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r93\">2004</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r23\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r23\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r24\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r24\">2012a</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r13\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r13\">2009</a>; Brada\u010d et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r21\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r21\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r22\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r22\">2012</a>; Maizy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r108\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r108\">2010</a>; Richard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r137\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r137\">2011</a>; Hall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r74\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r74\">2012</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r178\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r178\">2012a</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r170\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r170\">2012</a>; Zackrisson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r173\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r173\">2012</a>; and for a recent review, see Kneib &amp; Natarajan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r96\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r96\">2011</a>). The drawbacks of lensed searches are reduced search area in the magnified source planes and some uncertainty in the estimate of that search area introduced by the lens modeling. But the rewards are galaxies that are strongly magnified, often by factors of 10 or more. Lensed searches are significantly more efficient in yielding high-redshift candidates bright enough for spectroscopic confirmation, including A1703-zD6 (Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r24\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r24\">2012a</a>) at <em>z</em> = 7.045 (Schenker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r146\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r146\">2012</a>).</p><p>The Cluster Lensing and Supernova survey with <em>Hubble</em> (CLASH; Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r131\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r131\">2012</a>) is a large <em>Hubble</em> program imaging 25 galaxy clusters in 16 filters, including five in the near infrared (0.9\u20131.7\u2009\u03bcm). Five of these, including MACSJ0647.7+7015 (<em>z</em> = 0.591; Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r54\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r54\">2007</a>), were selected on the basis of their especially strong gravitational lensing power as observed in previous imaging, with the primary goal of discovering highly magnified galaxies at high redshift. To date, some of the more notable strongly lensed galaxies found in CLASH include a doubly imaged galaxy with a spectroscopic redshift of <em>z</em> = 6.027 observed at ~24.6\u2009mag (Richard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r137\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r137\">2011</a>), which is possibly ~800\u00a0Myr old (although see Pirzkal et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r129\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r129\">2012</a>); a quadruply imaged <em>z</em> ~ 6.2 galaxy observed at 24th magnitude (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r178\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r178\">2012a</a>); and the <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 candidate galaxy MACS1149-JD observed at ~25.7\u2009mag (Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>). The <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 candidate is strongly lensed by MACSJ1149.6+2223, another CLASH cluster selected for its high magnification strength.</p><p>Here we report the discovery of MACS0647-JD, a candidate for the earliest galaxy yet detected at a redshift of <em>z</em> = 10.7<sup>+0.6</sup><sub>\u22120.4</sub> (95% confidence), just 427<sup>\u221230</sup><sub>+21</sub> million years after the big bang. It is strongly lensed by MACSJ0647.7+7015, yielding three multiple images observed at F160W AB mag ~25.9, 26.1, and 27.3, magnified by factors of ~8, 7, and 2. The brightest image is similar in flux to MACS1149-JD (F160W mag ~25.7) at <em>z</em> = 9.6 \u00b1 0.2 (68% confidence) and roughly 15\u00a0times (3\u2009mag) brighter than the <em>z</em> = 10.3 \u00b1 0.8 (68% confidence) candidate in the UDF (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>).</p><p>MACS0647-JD is a <em>J</em>-dropout as all three lensed images are securely detected in F160W and F140W but drop out of detection in the <em>J</em>-band F125W and all 14 bluer <em>HST</em> filters. We show this photometry is most likely due to the Lyman-\u03b1 break redshifted to ~1.46\u2009\u03bcm at <em>z</em> ~ 11. This Lyman dropout technique (Meier <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r113\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r113\">1976</a>; Giavalisco <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r67\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r67\">2002</a>) pioneered by Steidel et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r157\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r157\">1996</a>) at <em>z</em> ~ 3 has been used with a high success rate to identify high-redshift candidates later spectroscopically confirmed out to <em>z</em> ~ 7. However, care must be taken not to confuse dropouts with intrinsically red (evolved and/or dusty) galaxies at intermediate redshift (Schaerer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r144\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r144\">2007</a>; Dunlop et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r53\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r53\">2007</a>; Chary et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r36\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r36\">2007</a>; Capak et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r33\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r33\">2011</a>; Boone et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r12\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r12\">2011</a>; Hayes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>). In the case of MACS0647-JD, we show that it is extremely difficult, if not impossible, for low-redshift interlopers to reproduce the observed colors, especially the <em>J</em><sub>125</sub> \u2212 <em>H</em><sub>160</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 3 magnitude break of MACS0647-JD. We also test our analysis method by reanalyzing previously published <em>J</em>-dropouts that later proved to be at intermediate redshift. Our Bayesian photometric redshift (BPZ; Ben\u00edtez <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r9\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r9\">2000</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r38\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r38\">2006</a>) analysis correctly shows that intermediate redshift solutions are preferred for those objects, while higher redshift solutions are preferred for MACS0647-JD.</p><p>We describe our <em>HST</em> and <em>Spitzer Space Telescope</em> <em>(Spitzer)</em> observations in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s2\">2</a> and present photometry in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s3\">3</a>. We derive the photometric redshift in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a> and consider a wide range of possible interlopers in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5\">5</a>. We present our gravitational lensing analysis in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6\">6</a>. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7\">7</a>, we derive physical properties of MACS0647-JD based on additional photometric analysis. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s8\">8</a>, we compare our observed number density of <em>z</em> ~ 11 galaxies to that expected, and we constrain the <em>z</em> &gt; 9 SFRD. Finally, we present conclusions in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s9\">9</a>.</p><p>Where necessary, we assume a concordance \u039bCDM cosmology with <em>h</em> = 0.7, \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub> = 0.3, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.7, where <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 100\u2009<em>h</em>\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. In this cosmology, 1'' = 3.93 kpc at <em>z</em> = 10.8 and 6.62\u00a0kpc at the cluster redshift <em>z</em> = 0.591.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s2\" name=\"apj451260s2\">2.\u00a0OBSERVATIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>As part of the CLASH program, <em>HST</em> observed the core of MACSJ0647.7+7015 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f1\">1</a>) during 19 orbits spread among eight different visits between 2011 October 5 and November 29 (General Observer program 12101). Imaging was obtained with the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3; Kimble et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r90\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r90\">2008</a>) and Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS; Ford et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r61\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r61\">2003</a>) in 15 filters spanning 0.2\u20131.7\u2009\u03bcm, including five near-infrared WFC3/IR filters spanning 0.9\u20131.7\u2009\u03bcm. These data sets were supplemented by prior ACS imaging obtained in the F555W (0.56\u2009\u03bcm) and F814W (0.81\u2009\u03bcm) filters to total depths of ~3.5 and 5.5 orbits, respectively (GO 9722 P.I. Ebeling; GO 10493, 10793 P.I. Gal-Yam). These observations are detailed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t1\">1</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Lenstool strong lensing mass model of MACSJ0647.7+7015 and multiply imaged galaxies as identified in this work using the Zitrin et al. class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s3-1\">3.1</a>).</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We processed the images for debias, (super)flats, and darks using standard techniques, then co-aligned and combined them to a scale of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />065\u00a0pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>; see Koekemoer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r99\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r99\">2002</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r98\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r98\">2011</a>) for further information on the astrometric alignment and drizzle algorithms that were used and Postman et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r131\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r131\">2012</a>) for specific details on their implementation in CLASH. We also produced inverse variance maps (IVMs) based on the observed sky level, identified cosmic rays, detector flat field, read noise, dark current, and bad pixels. These IVMs may be used to estimate the level of uncertainty in each pixel before accounting for correlated noise and any Poisson source noise.</p><p>Imaging at longer wavelengths was obtained by <em>Spitzer</em> with the InfraRed Array Camera (IRAC; Fazio et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r55\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r55\">2004</a>) ch1 (3.6\u2009\u03bcm) and ch2 (4.5\u2009\u03bcm) with total exposure times of 5 hr at each wavelength (program 60034, P.I. Egami). These observations were divided into two epochs separated by ~5.5 months (2009 November 10 and 2010 April 23). We combined the Basic Calibrated Data (BCD) using MOPEX (Makovoz &amp; Khan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r109\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r109\">2005</a>) to produce mosaicked images.</p><p>As of 2012 July, CLASH had obtained 16-band <em>HST</em> observations for 17 clusters, including MACSJ0647.7+7015 and three other \"high-magnification\" strong lensing clusters, as given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t2\">2</a>. We searched for high-redshift galaxies in the WFC3/IR fields of view (FOVs) of all 17 of these clusters (L. D. Bradley et al. 2013, in preparation).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj451260t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 2.</b>\u00a0Seventeen Clusters Searched in This The latter \"high magnification\" clusters are marked with Y's here. For details, see Postman et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r131\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r131\">2012</a>).\n<a name=\"apj451260t2fn2\" id=\"apj451260t2fn2\"></a><sup> b</sup>RA and decl. (J2000) are given in parentheses for the Abell clusters, encoded as they are in the names of the other clusters.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t2_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Out of ~20,000 detected sources, we identified MACS0647-JD (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f2\">2</a>) as having an exceptionally high photometric redshift (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a>). Our selection was based on spectral energy distribution (SED) fitting as used in some previous high-redshift searches (e.g., McLure et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r111\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r111\">2006</a>; Dunlop et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r53\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r53\">2007</a>; Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r58\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r58\">2010</a>). We did not impose specific magnitude limits, color cuts, or other detection thresholds on our selection as in other works (e.g., Bunker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r30\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r30\">2010</a>; Yan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r172\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r172\">2011</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r123\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r123\">2012b</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Three images of MACS0647-JD as observed in various filters with <em>HST</em>. The leftmost panels show the summed 11 hr (17-orbit) exposures obtained in eight filters spanning 0.4\u20130.9\u2009\u03bcm with the Advanced Camera for Surveys. The five middle columns show observations with the Wide Field Camera 3 IR channel in F105W, F110W, F125W, F140W, and F160W, all shown with the same linear scale in electrons per second. The F125W images were obtained at a single roll angle, and a small region near JD2 was affected by persistence due to a moderately bright star in our parallel observations immediately prior (see also Figure <a href=\"#apj451260f4\">4</a>). The right panels zoom in by a factor of 2 to show F110W+F140W+F160W color images scaled linearly between 0 and 0.1 \u03bcJy.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s3\" name=\"apj451260s3\">3.\u00a0PHOTOMETRY</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apj451260s3-1\" name=\"apj451260s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0<em>HST</em> Photometry</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><h4 id=\"apj451260s3-1-1\" name=\"apj451260s3-1-1\">3.1.1.\u00a0Photometric Analysis</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We used SExtractor version 2.5.0 (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r10\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r10\">1996</a>) to detect objects in a weighted sum of all five <em>HST</em> WFC3/IR images. Along the edge of each object, SExtractor defines an isophotal aperture consisting of pixels with values above a detection threshold. We set this threshold equal to the rms measured locally near each object. Isophotal fluxes (and magnitudes) are measured within these isophotal apertures. SExtractor derives flux uncertainties by adding in quadrature the background rms derived from our inverse variance maps and the Poisson uncertainty from the object flux.</p><p>Since our images are drizzled to a 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />065\u00a0pixel scale, which is 2\u20133\u00a0times smaller than the WFC3 point-spread function (PSF), the resulting images contain significant correlated noise. The weight maps produced by drizzle represent the expected variance in the absence of correlated noise. To account for the correlated noise, one may apply a correction factor as in Casertano et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r34\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r34\">2000</a>).</p><p>Previous authors have also noted that SExtractor tends to underestimate flux uncertainties (Feldmeier et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r56\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r56\">2002</a>; Labb\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r101\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r101\">2003</a>; Gawiser et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r66\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r66\">2006</a>) by as much as a factor of 2\u20133 (Becker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r6\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r6\">2007</a>). In this work, we obtained empirical measurements of the flux uncertainties using the following method which also captures the effects of correlated noise.</p><p>SExtractor has the ability to measure the local background within a rectangular annulus (default width 24 pixels) around each object. We constructed a rectangle of the same size, but rather than calculate the rms of the individual pixels, we obtained samples of the background flux within this region using the isophotal aperture shifted to new positions. In other words, we moved the isophotal aperture to every position within this rectangle, sampling the flux at each position. We discarded measurements for which the aperture includes part of any object, as we are interested in measuring the background flux. Finally, we measured the rms of these measurements and added in quadrature the object's Poisson uncertainty to obtain the total flux uncertainty for that object. We found that this technique indeed yielded larger flux uncertainties than reported by SExtractor, typically by factors of 2\u20133 in the WFC3/IR filters and by lower factors in ACS and WFC3/UVIS.</p><p>We also used this method to determine object fluxes. The mean of the flux measurements in the nearby apertures was adopted as the local flux bias, which we subtracted from the flux measurement in the object itself. We found that this yielded photometry very similar to that obtained using SExtractor, agreeing well within the photometric uncertainties. While we used this photometry for all subsequent analyses, we also verified that our derived photometric redshifts did not vary significantly (after excluding the F110W second epoch exposures; Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s3-1-3\">3.1.3</a>) if we instead utilized photometry derived directly from SExtractor.</p><p>We corrected for Galactic extinction of <em>E</em>(<em>B</em> \u2212 <em>V</em>) = 0.11 in the direction of MACSJ0647.7+7015 as derived using the Schlegel et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r149\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r149\">1998</a>) IR dust emission maps. For each filter, the magnitudes of extinction per unit <em>E</em>(<em>B</em> \u2212 <em>V</em>) are given in Postman et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r131\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r131\">2012</a>, their Table 5). (These values should be ~10% lower in the NUV and optical according to Schlafly &amp; Finkbeiner <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r148\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r148\">2011</a>.) This extinction reddens the observed colors at the few percent level in the near-IR. Thus, the effects on the <em>J</em>-dropout are negligible. The extinctions range from 0.05 to 0.11\u2009mag in the WFC3/IR images; 0.16 to 0.46\u2009mag in the ACS images; and 0.50 to 0.83\u2009mag in WFC3/UVIS. We note that the extinction may be somewhat uncertain due to patchy galactic cirrus in the direction of MACSJ0647.7+7015.</p></div><h4 id=\"apj451260s3-1-2\" name=\"apj451260s3-1-2\">3.1.2.\u00a0Photometric Results</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our resulting 17-band <em>HST</em> photometry is given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t3\">3</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f3\">3</a>. All three <em>J</em>-dropouts are detected at &gt;10\u03c3 in F160W, &gt;6\u03c3 in F140W, and &lt;3\u03c3 in all other filters. JD is not detected above 1\u03c3 in any filter blueward of F140W.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Observed <em>HST</em> photometry (filled circles and triangles) plotted against the expected fluxes (open blue squares) from a young starburst galaxy spectrum (gray line) redshifted to <em>z</em> ~ 11. <em>HST</em> filter transmission curves are plotted in the upper panel, normalized to their maxima, and with black dots indicating the effective \"pivot\" wavelengths. Photometry of the <em>J</em>-dropouts observed through these filters (Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t3\">3</a>) is plotted as the larger circles and triangles for positive and negative observed fluxes, respectively, with 1\u03c3 error bars. For some points, horizontal \"error bars\" are plotted to reiterate the filter widths. The gray line is a model spectrum of a young starburst at <em>z</em> = 11.0, the best fit to the summed photometry. The integrals of this spectrum through our filters give the model predicted fluxes plotted as blue squares. Other galaxy types at <em>z</em> ~ 11 yield similar predicted <em>HST</em> fluxes, as the shape of the spectrum cannot be constrained by the <em>HST</em> photometry alone. Redshifted Lyman-\u03b1 at 0.1216\u2009\u03bcm(1 + <em>z</em>) ~ 1.46\u2009\u03bcm is indicated by the vertical dashed line.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span Our empirical rederivations of the photometric uncertainties, including proper accounting for correlated noise (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s3-1-1\">3.1.1</a>), reduced the significance of this detection to 2.5\u03c3. However, we ultimately we concluded that this marginal detection was completely spurious due to significantly elevated and non-Poissonian backgrounds due to earthshine in two out of five F110W exposures, both obtained during the second epoch (see below). After excluding these exposures, the detection significance drops to 0.3\u03c3, consistent with background noise. For reference, see the WFC3/IR images in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f2\">2</a>.</p><p>Even based on the initial \"standard\" analysis described above, we determined that MACS0647-JD is at <em>z</em> &lt; 9 with a likelihood of ~10<sup>\u22129</sup> based on a joint photometric redshift analysis of all three images (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a>). This likelihood decreased further to 3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup> based on our improved analysis. These values are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t4\">4</a>. The spuriously high flux measurements may be seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f5\">5</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj451260t4\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 4.</b>\u00a0Effects of F110W Aberrant Second Epoch</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260t4\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t4_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 4\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\"><th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">F110W detection \u03c3</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">JD1</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">JD2</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">JD3</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>P</em>(<em>z</em> &lt; 9)<sup>a</sup></th></tr>\n</thead><tbody><tr valign=\"top\"><td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">SExtractor photometry</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">0.9</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">5.0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">1.7</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">1 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22129</sup></td></tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\"><td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">Empirical uncertainties</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">0.5</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">2.5</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">1.0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">4 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22128</sup></td></tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\"><td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">Excluding second epoch</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u22120.8</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">0.3</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">1.9</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Notes.</strong> JD2 is spuriously detected in F110W images processed using standard techniques. This is due to significantly elevated non-Poissonian backgrounds in the second epoch of observations due to earthshine. We exclude this epoch in our analysis. See Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s3-1\">3.1</a>\n<a name=\"apj451260t4fn1\" id=\"apj451260t4fn1\"></a><sup> a</sup>Based on the summed photometry of all three images, and assuming that MACS0647-JD is a galaxy well described by our templates. See Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5\">5</a>.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t4_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t4_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>The final observations of MACSJ0647.7+7015 were two 502\u00a0s exposures in F110W obtained during visit A9, the second epoch for that filter. We found these to have significantly elevated backgrounds of 1.9 (\u03c3-clipped mean) \u00b10.44 (rms) and 6.4 \u00b1 0.27 electrons s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively, compared with the more typical values around 1.5 \u00b1 0.08. These high backgrounds were due to earthshine, or sunlight reflected from the Earth. The first observation was obtained during twilight as the telescope pointed within 67\u00b0\u201359\u00b0 of the bright limb of Earth. This Earth limb angle continued to steadily decrease from 47\u00b0 to 24\u00b0 during the second observation which was obtained during daylight. In the observation log, the diagnostic Earth bright limb flag was raised halfway through the second exposure. We also examined the 10 individual readouts of 100\u00a0s each obtained over the course of both exposures and found that the mean background increased steadily from 0.9 to 7.5 electrons s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The resulting elevated background rms values of 0.44 and 0.27 electrons s<sup>\u22121</sup> in the two exposures are the highest and sixth highest relative to the median values for a given filter in 1582 CLASH observations to date of 17 clusters. None of the three F160W observations obtained at the beginning of visit A9 exhibit elevated backgrounds because they were obtained at night (twilight had yet to set in) and Earth is less bright in F160W.</p><p>Specifically, when we compared the measured rms values to what would be expected from scaling the background intensity levels, we found that these rms values are several times higher than would be expected in the case of Poissonian statistics. We attribute this to the fact that the sky background was increasing in a strongly nonlinear fashion during the exposure, whereas the up-the-ramp slope fitting algorithm implemented in \"calwf3\" implicitly assumes that the count rate is constant when converting measured counts into counts per second (see Dressel <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r50\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r50\">2011</a>). Since this assumption is violated, the pixel-to-pixel variations in the final count-rate image no longer scale as expected for Poissonian statistics, as demonstrated by the much higher rms values. Since these data no longer conform to Poissonian statistics, we were able to demonstrate that attempting to combine them with the other data did not yield an improvement in signal-to-noise ratio but instead produced combined data sets with non-Poissonian statistics, from which we were not able to obtain reliable photometry.</p><p>We therefore exclude the two F110W visit A9 exposures from our analysis and derive photometry instead from the weighted sum of the three visit A2 exposures.</p></div></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s3-2\" name=\"apj451260s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0<em>Spitzer</em> Photometry</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>To derive photometry in the longer wavelength <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC images (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f7\">7</a>), we performed both GALFIT PSF fitting and aperture photometry on JD2 and JD3. No significant flux is detected for either object in either channel except for a 3\u03c3 detection of JD2 in ch2: mag = 24.8 \u00b1 0.3. Aperture photometry (2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4 diameter aperture) yields mag = 25.8 \u00b1 0.3, subject to an approximate 0.7\u2009mag correction, roughly consistent with the GALFIT-derived photometry.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0<em>Spitzer</em> IRAC ch1 (3.6\u2009\u03bcm) and ch2 (4.5\u2009\u03bcm) images of MACS0647-JD compared to the <em>HST</em> WFC3/IR F160W (1.6\u2009\u03bcm) image. Two intensity scalings and zooms are shown. Left: both 26'' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 26'' F160W cutouts are scaled linearly in photon counts to the same range as in Figure <a href=\"#apj451260f2\">2</a>. And for each <em>Spitzer</em> filter, the same count range is used in each row. The background photon counts are significantly higher near JD1 and JD2 (top row) due to intracluster light and scattered starlight. MACS0647-JD is not detected brightly in the <em>Spitzer</em> images, supporting the high-redshift solution. The only possible detection we report is for JD2 at 3.1\u03c3 in ch2 (Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t3\">3</a>). JD1 is contaminated by light from other nearby galaxies which we modeled and subtracted to estimate JD's photometry. Right: in each of these 5'' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 5'' closeups, the intensity is scaled independently to the observed range within the central 3'' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 3''.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>JD1 is significantly contaminated by light from a nearby cluster galaxy. We modeled this galaxy using GALFIT, subtracted it from the image, and measured photometry in 2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4 diameter apertures, yielding a null detection plus uncertainty. We also added a simulated 25th magnitude source and used GALFIT to derive its photometry. We conservatively combined the uncertainties from these two measurements in quadrature to yield total uncertainties (1\u03c3 upper limits) of 277 and 245 nJy in ch1 and ch2, respectively (3\u03c3 limits of mag 24.2 and 24.1). We also experimented with inflating these uncertainties further by 1\u2009mag (3\u03c3 limits of 23.2 and 23.1\u2009mag). This would increase the JD1 <em>z</em> &lt; 9 likelihood (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a>) from ~3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22127</sup> to 2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22125</sup>, and the likelihood based on the integrated photometry of all three images from 3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup> to 2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22129</sup>.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s4\" name=\"apj451260s4\">4.\u00a0PHOTOMETRIC REDSHIFT</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We perform two independent analyses of the <em>HST</em>+<em>Spitzer</em> photometry to estimate the photometric redshift of MACS0647-JD. These two methods, BPZ (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4-1\">4.1</a>) and LePHARE (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4-2\">4.2</a>), were the top 2 performers out of 17 methods tested in Hildebrandt et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r76\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r76\">2010</a>). They yielded the most accurate redshifts with the fewest outliers given a photometric catalog for galaxies with known spectroscopic redshifts.</p><p>According to our gravitational lensing models (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6\">6</a>), MACS0647-JD1, 2, and 3 are likely three multiple images of the same strongly lensed background galaxy. Thus, in this section, we present photometric redshift likelihoods for each individual image, as well as jointly for the two brighter images and for all three images.</p><h3 id=\"apj451260s4-1\" name=\"apj451260s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Bayesian Photometric Redshifts (BPZ)</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We used BPZ (Ben\u00edtez <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r9\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r9\">2000</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r38\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r38\">2006</a>) for our primary photometric redshift analyses. We modeled the observed <em>HST</em>+<em>Spitzer</em> photometry of MACS0647-JD using model SEDs from PEGASE (Fioc &amp; Rocca-Volmerange <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r60\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r60\">1997</a>), which have been significantly adjusted and recalibrated to match the observed photometry of galaxies with known spectroscopic redshifts from FIREWORKS (Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r171\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r171\">2008</a>). The FIREWORKS data set includes 0.38\u201324\u2009\u03bcm photometry of galaxies down to mag ~24.3 (5\u03c3 <em>K</em> band) and spectroscopic redshifts out to <em>z</em> ~ 3.7. In analyses of large data sets with high quality spectra, this template set yields <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />1% outliers, demonstrating that it encompasses the range of metallicities, extinctions, and star formation histories (SFHs) observed for the vast majority of real galaxies. (In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-1\">5.1</a>, we explore a still broader range of galaxy properties using a synthetic template set that has not been recalibrated to match observed galaxy colors.) These templates include nebular emission lines as implemented by Fioc &amp; Rocca-Volmerange (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r60\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r60\">1997</a>).</p><p>The Bayesian analysis tempers the SED model quality of fit with an empirically derived prior <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>, <em>T</em>|<em>m</em>) on the galaxy redshift and type given its (delensed) magnitude. Our prior was constructed as in Ben\u00edtez (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r9\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r9\">2000</a>) and updated based on likelihoods <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>, <em>T</em>|<em>m</em>) observed in COSMOS (Ilbert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r80\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r80\">2009</a>), GOODS-MUSIC (Grazian et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r70\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r70\">2006</a>; Santini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r142\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r142\">2009</a>), and the UDF (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r38\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r38\">2006</a>). According to this prior (extrapolated to higher redshifts), all galaxy types of intrinsic (delensed) magnitude ~28.2 are over 80\u00a0times less likely to be at <em>z</em> ~ 11 than <em>z</em> ~ 2. Thus, our analysis is more conservative regarding high-redshift candidates than an analysis which neglects to implement such a prior (implicitly assuming a flat prior in redshift). The prior likelihoods for MACS0647-JD are uncertain both due to the prior's extrapolation to <em>z</em> ~ 11 and uncertainty in MACS0647-JD's intrinsic (delensed) magnitude. Yet, it serves as a useful approximation that is surely more accurate than a flat prior.</p><p>Based on this analysis, we derived photometric redshift likelihood distributions as plotted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f8\">8</a> and summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t5\">5</a>. The images JD1, JD2, and JD3 are best fit by a starburst SED at <em>z</em> ~ 10.9, 11.0, and 10.1, respectively. After applying the Bayesian prior, we find that JD1 and JD2 are most likely starbursts at <em>z</em> ~ 10.6 and 11.0, respectively. A <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 elliptical template is slightly preferred for JD3; however, <em>z</em> = 11 is within the 99% confidence limits (CLs). Observed at ~27.3\u2009mag, we may not expect this fainter image to yield as reliable a photometric redshift.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Top row: photometric redshift probability distributions based on our BPZ analysis (Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4-1\">4.1</a>) of <em>HST</em>+<em>Spitzer</em> photometry for each image. Cumulative probabilities <em>P</em>(&lt; <em>z</em>) are shaded gray; probabilities <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>) per unit 0.01 in redshift are drawn as black lines; and likelihoods for individual SED templates are drawn as colored lines. In this work, we use four elliptical templates (Ell), one E/S0, two spirals (Sbc and Scd), and four starbursts (SB). We then interpolate nine templates between each pair of adjacent templates. Bottom left: joint likelihoods for all three images. Bottom center: likelihoods based on the summed photometry of all three images. For all of these likelihoods, we assume the prior plotted at bottom right for galaxies of an intrinsic (delensed) magnitude of ~28.2. This prior was empirically derived from large surveys with photometric and spectroscopic redshifts and extrapolated to higher redshifts.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download
