0.15</td></tr></tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Note.</strong>\n<a name=\"apj451260t6fn1\" id=\"apj451260t6fn1\"></a><sup> a</sup>Joint likelihoods for each system with approximate 68% uncertainties.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t6_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t6_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>For each of systems 3, 5, and 8, our lens models predict a third faint counterimage, but we cannot unambiguously identify it among several possible candidates. To be conservative, we do not include these uncertain identifications as constraints on our lens models. Inclusion of these candidates does not significantly affect the lens models. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f1\">1</a>, we indicate the predicted locations of these counterimages.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s6-2\" name=\"apj451260s6-2\">6.2.\u00a0Strong Lens Modeling Methods</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Based on the observed positions of all 24 strongly lensed images, we model the mass of MACSJ0647.7+7015 using three different methods: the Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r177\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r177\">2009</a>) method, Lenstool (Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r95\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r95\">1993</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r83\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r83\">2007</a>), and LensPerfect (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r39\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r39\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r37\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r37\">2010</a>). The first two methods are \"parametric\" in that they assume light traces mass, which has proved to be a very good prior. For example, some of the earliest efforts to model cluster lenses found that assigning masses to individual luminous cluster galaxies significantly improved the reproduction of strongly lensed images (Kassiola et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r86\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r86\">1992</a>; Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r94\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r94\">1996</a>). LensPerfect makes no assumptions about light tracing mass, exploring a broader range of mass models and perfectly reproducing the observed positions of all multiple images positions as input.</p><p>The Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r177\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r177\">2009</a>) mass model parameterization consists of three components: the cluster galaxies, a smooth cluster halo, and an external shear. Cluster galaxies were identified according to the \"red sequence\" in F814W\u2013F555W color\u2013magnitude space, then verified with photometric redshifts. Each cluster galaxy was modeled as a power-law density profile, its mass scaling with flux observed in F814W. In this work, we also allowed the masses of the two brightest central galaxies to vary independently. The cluster halo component was derived from this galaxy component by smoothing the latter with either a polynomial spline or a Gaussian. The two components were allowed to scale independently before being added. In all, there were eight free parameters: the mass scalings of the galaxy and halo components, the masses of the two brightest central galaxies, the power law of the galaxy density profiles, the degree of the polynomial spline or Gaussian smoothing width, and the amplitude and direction of the external shear.</p><p>The Lenstool model consisted of an ellipsoidal Navarro\u2013Frenk\u2013White halo (Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r119\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r119\">1996</a>) plus cluster galaxies modeled as truncated pseudo-isothermal elliptical mass distributions (PIEMDs; Kassiola &amp; Kovner <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r85\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r85\">1993</a>). We assumed core radii <em>r</em><sub>core</sub> = 300 pc and luminosity scaling relations as in Jullo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r83\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r83\">2007</a>): velocity dispersion \u03c3<sub>0</sub>\u221d<em>L</em><sup>1/4</sup> and cutoff radius <em>r</em><sub>cut</sub>\u221d<em>L</em><sup>1/2</sup>, resulting in all galaxies having equal mass-to-light ratios. The normalizations of these two scaling relations were free parameters along with the cluster halo position (<em>x</em>, <em>y</em>), ellipticity (<em>e</em>, \u03b8), scale radius, and concentration. There were eight free parameters in all.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s6-3\" name=\"apj451260s6-3\">6.3.\u00a0Lens Model Results</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Given the observed position of any one of the MACS0647-JD images, the Lenstool model accurately predicts and reproduces the positions of the other two images to an rms of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3, as minimized for <em>z</em> = 11.59<sup>+0.12</sup><sub>\u22121.53</sub>. This scatter is consistent with ~1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4 expected due to lensing by line-of-sight structures and variation in the mass-to-light ratio of cluster galaxies (Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r84\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r84\">2010</a>; Host <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r77\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r77\">2012</a>).</p><p>The lens model and inferred redshift for MACS0647-JD do not change significantly if the MACS0647-JD images are excluded as constraints. In this case, the eight-parameter lens model remains well constrained by the 21 other multiple images which provide 26 constraints (see discussion below).</p><p>Using the Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r177\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r177\">2009</a>) method, two sets of acceptable models are found in different regions of the model parameter space. One set prefers <em>z</em> ~ 11 for MACS0647-JD, while the other prefers <em>z</em> ~ 3.5. The latter mass models have flatter profiles.</p><p>Our LensPerfect analysis confirms that this is a degeneracy between the MACS0647-JD redshift and the cluster mass distribution. A wide range of redshifts including <em>z</em> = 3.5, 11.0, and 11.6 is permitted given the strong lensing data. When fixing the redshift to any of these values, LensPerfect produces reasonable lens models (physical and with light approximately tracing mass) that perfectly reproduce all 24 observed positions of the nine strongly lensed galaxies. When the MACS0647-JD redshift is set lower, the cluster mass distribution is more spread out yielding a flatter profile. We confirm that the parametric models have similar differences, in part due to their parameterizations of the cluster mass distribution.</p><p>Including the redshift of MACS0647-JD, both parametric models have <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 9 free parameters. (This number should be considered a maximum given covariances among the parameters. See discussion in Andrae et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r2\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r2\">2010</a>.) There are 30 constraints =2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> (24 \u2212 9), where the constraints are the two coordinates (<em>x</em>,<em>y</em>) from each of the 24 multiple images minus the nine unknown source positions. Thus, each model has <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 21 degrees of freedom (30 constraints \u2212 9 parameters). The Lenstool model reproduces all lensed image positions to an rms of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />17. Assuming a scatter of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4 as explained above, this yields \u03c7<sup>2</sup> \u2248 24 <b>&#x00d7;</b> (1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />17/1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4)<sup>2</sup> = 16.8 with 21 degrees of freedom, for a reduced \u03c7<sup>2</sup><sub>\u03bd</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 16.8/21 \u2248 0.8.</p><p>The Zitrin spline model with the flat mass profile preferring <em>z</em> ~ 3.5 for MACS0647-JD obtains an rms of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 for \u03c7<sup>2</sup> \u2248 15, also with 21 degrees of freedom, yielding \u03c7<sup>2</sup><sub>\u03bd</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.7. When the models are forced to adopt <em>z</em> ~ 11, the best fit is found with a Gaussian-smoothed model, yielding an rms of 2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9, for \u03c7<sup>2</sup> \u2248 103 and a reduced \u03c7<sup>2</sup><sub>\u03bd</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 4.9.</p><p>Assuming MACS0647-JD is at <em>z</em> = 11.0, the Lenstool model (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f1\">1</a>) estimates magnifications of ~8.4, 6.6, and 2.8 for JD1, JD2, and JD3, respectively, with uncertainties of ~20%. These magnifications are consistent with an F160W =20 \u00b1 4\u00a0nJy (28.2 \u00b1 0.2\u2009mag) source magnified by factors of ~ 8.1, 6.8, and 2.1 to match the observed F160W fluxes within their ~10% uncertainties (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t3\">3</a>).</p><p>LensPerfect models perfectly reproduce all 24 observed image positions as input. These data constrain the mass distribution and profile well globally but only to a resolution of ~20'', or ~130\u00a0kpc, roughly the average separation between the strongly lensed images. This resolution is insufficient to obtain robust estimates of the image magnifications, which are strong functions of local mass gradients. Here these magnifications are better estimated by adopting priors of light tracing mass as in the parametric methods.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s7\" name=\"apj451260s7\">7.\u00a0PHYSICAL PROPERTIES OF MACS0647-JD</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We estimate the rest-frame UV luminosity and SFR (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-1\">7.1</a>), infer a rough stellar mass (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-2\">7.2</a>), and place upper limits on the physical size (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-4\">7.4</a>). In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-3\">7.3</a>, we explore SED parameter degeneracies and place modest constraints on other properties of MACS0647-JD.</p><h3 id=\"apj451260s7-1\" name=\"apj451260s7-1\">7.1.\u00a0Rest-frame UV Luminosity; Star Formation Rate</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As described above, we estimate the intrinsic (unlensed) magnitude of MACS0647-JD to be 28.2 \u00b1 0.2 in F160W. At <em>z</em> ~ 11, the Lyman\u03b1 break falls within the F160W bandpass, attenuating the observed flux; the rest-frame UV (0.16\u00a0\u03bcm) continuum flux is ~0.25\u00a0mag brighter. To convert this flux to rest-frame UV absolute magnitude <em>M</em><sub>UV, AB</sub>, we add three terms. Most significantly, the magnitude is brighter by the distance modulus ~50.3. The flux per unit frequency is also dimmer by a factor of 1 + <em>z</em> (~2.7\u00a0mag) simply because the rest frame samples a higher frequency. We also derive a small color term of ~0.1\u00a0mag as we switch from the blueshifted F160W filter (~0.13\u2009\u03bcm) to a tophat filter centered on 0.16\u00a0\u03bcm for comparison with previous measurements. Combining these terms, we find <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> ~ \u221219.5. Converting this to UV luminosity at a distance of 10\u00a0pc, we find <em>L</em><sub>UV</sub> ~ 2.8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>28</sup> erg s<sup>\u22121</sup> Hz<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p><p>This rest-frame UV luminosity can be generated by an SFR of ~4\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup> assuming a Salpeter (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r141\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r141\">1955</a>) initial mass function (IMF) with mass limits 0.1\u2013100\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (Kennicutt <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r88\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r88\">1998</a>). The ionizing efficiency could be increased by a factor of ~1.8 for a Chabrier (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r35\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r35\">2003</a>) IMF or ~3 for a top-heavy IMF (Bruzual &amp; Charlot <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r29\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r29\">2003</a>; Schaerer <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r143\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r143\">2003</a>; Stiavelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r159\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r159\">2004</a>), with the latter generally realized in simulations of high-redshift galaxies (e.g., Abel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r1\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r1\">2002</a>; Bromm et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r26\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r26\">2002</a>). Stellar rotations may also increase this efficiency by a factor of ~2\u20135 (Levesque et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r107\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r107\">2012</a>). Given these and other uncertainties, an SFR of ~1\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> or lower could generate <em>L</em><sub>UV</sub> derived for MACS0647-JD.</p><p>This luminosity <em>L</em><sub>UV</sub> is ~<em>L</em>* or perhaps a few times brighter than <em>L</em>*, depending on which extrapolation we assume to estimate this characteristic luminosity at <em>z</em> ~ 11 (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r15\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r15\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>; Robertson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r139\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r139\">2010</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>). Based on the estimated luminosity function (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s8\">8</a>) and our lens magnification model, we find that a <em>z</em> ~ 11 galaxy lensed to 26th magnitude does in fact have an ~80% likelihood of being intrinsically brighter than <em>L</em>*.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s7-2\" name=\"apj451260s7-2\">7.2.\u00a0Stellar Mass</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Meaningful observational constraints on the stellar mass of MACS0647-JD would require rest-frame optical photometry redward of 0.4\u2009\u03bcm (beyond the Balmer and 4000\u2009\u00c5 breaks), or 4.8\u2009\u03bcm observed. However, we may infer a stellar mass estimate as follows.</p><p>Specific star formation rates (sSFRs) of 2\u20133\u00a0Gyr<sup>\u22121</sup> (that is, 2\u20133\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> formed per year per 10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> total stellar mass) are observed on average for galaxies over a remarkably broad range of redshifts (2 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 7; see, e.g., Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r156\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r156\">2009</a>; Gonz\u00e1lez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r69\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r69\">2010</a>; McLure et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r112\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r112\">2011</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>). If this \"plateau\" continues out to <em>z</em> ~ 11 and MACS0647-JD has a typical sSFR of ~2 or 3\u00a0Gyr<sup>\u22121</sup>, then this combined with our derived SFR would imply a stellar mass on the order of ~10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. The average stellar mass of <em>L</em>* galaxies was ~10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> at <em>z</em> ~ 7\u20138 and rose to a few times 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> by <em>z</em> ~ 2 (Gonz\u00e1lez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r69\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r69\">2010</a>; Labb\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r102\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r102\">2010</a>; Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r58\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r58\">2010</a>). Based on this trend, we may expect the average stellar mass of <em>L</em>* galaxies at <em>z</em> ~ 11 to be less than 10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. If this holds true for MACS0647-JD, it would suggest a higher sSFR, more in line with expectations from simulations that are in some tension (but perhaps only mild tension) with the observed sSFR plateau (e.g., Khochfar &amp; Silk <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r89\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r89\">2011</a>; Dav\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r47\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r47\">2011</a>; Weinmann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r167\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r167\">2011</a>; Behroozi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r7\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r7\">2012</a>).</p><p>We conclude that the stellar mass of MACS0647-JD is most likely on the order of 10<sup>8</sup>\u201310<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. The lower end of this mass range is more compatible with expectations from cosmological simulations and galaxy formation models. Based on simulations (e.g., Klypin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r92\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r92\">2011</a>), we may expect to find a dark matter halo of virial mass ~10<sup>10</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> or so within our search volume of a few times 1000 Mpc<sup>3</sup>. This would comfortably host a galaxy of stellar mass 10<sup>8</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> or so. A stellar mass of 10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> would be larger than expected.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s7-3\" name=\"apj451260s7-3\">7.3.\u00a0Other SED-based Constraints</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-1\">5.1</a>, we explored a broad range of galaxy properties to rule out lower redshift interlopers with a high degree of confidence. In this section, we quantify the degeneracies in those parameters (see also Pirzkal et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r129\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r129\">2012</a>; Pforr et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r128\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r128\">2012</a>) along with the modest constraints we obtain on them, assuming that MACS0647-JD is indeed at high redshift.</p><p>We modeled the observed photometry using the FSPS models from Conroy et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r42\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r42\">2009</a>) and Conroy &amp; Gunn (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r41\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r41\">2010</a>). As described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-1\">5.1</a>, we convolved their SSP models with a range of SFHs, including a single early burst, exponentially declining, constant, and exponentially rising. Though little dust is expected at these redshifts (e.g., Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>), we tested this assumption explicitly by adding a variable degree of Calzetti (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r31\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r31\">2001</a>) dust extinction. We did not add nebular emission lines. Only [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (3727\u2009\u00c5) redshifted to ~4.5\u2009\u03bcm might be a significant concern, though we only modestly detect a 4.5\u2009\u03bcm flux in JD2. We assume a flat linear prior in age up to the age of the universe at each redshift.</p><p>Our constraints on redshift, age, SFH (\u03c4), metallicity, and extinction are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f15\">15</a> based on the integrated photometry of all three images. We confirm that significant dust extinction is unlikely, with a rest-frame <em>V</em>-band extinction of <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> &lt; 0.25\u2009mag (95% confidence). Constraints on the other parameters are modest, with slight preferences for low metallicity and rising or continuous SFH in a maximally old galaxy (limited to <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />400\u00a0Myr by the age of the universe).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f15\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f15\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f15_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 15.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 15.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f15_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 15.</strong>\u00a0Constraints on redshift, age, star formation history (exponential scale factor \u03c4), metallicity (where <em>Z</em><sub>\u2609</sub> \u2248 0.019), and dust extinction (in <em>V</em>-band magnitudes). This is based on fitting the <em>HST</em>+<em>Spitzer</em> photometry integrated over the three images to the flexible stellar population synthesis models of Conroy et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r42\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r42\">2009</a>) and Conroy &amp; Gunn (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r41\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r41\">2010</a>) convolved with an exponential star formation history \u221dexp\u2009(\u2212 <em>t</em>/\u03c4) and with Calzetti (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r31\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r31\">2001</a>) dust extinction added (<em>R<sub>V</sub></em> = 4.05). The star formation history was either decaying (\u03c4/age = 0.3, 1, 3), rising (\u03c4/age = \u22120.3, \u22121, \u22123), constant star formation rate (CSF; \u03c4 = <b>&#x221e;</b>), or occurring in a single burst at \"birth\" (simple stellar population, SSP; \u03c4 = 0), with equal likelihoods for all eight possibilities. We assumed a flat linear prior for age (though it is plotted as log here) up to the age of the universe (as a function of redshift). Within each panel, the marginalized likelihood is plotted as a color map (scaled linearly) and confidence contours of 68% and 95% are overplotted as blue and white lines, respectively.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f15\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f15\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f15\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f15\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s7-4\" name=\"apj451260s7-4\">7.4.\u00a0Physical Size</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>After correcting for the observed PSF, JD1 and JD2 have observed half-light radii <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1, or delensed <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />03 (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.1 kpc). Based on extrapolations from lower redshifts (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r120\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r120\">2010a</a>; Mosleh et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r117\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r117\">2012</a>), we expect an average half-light radius of roughly \u3008<em>r</em><sub>1/2</sub>\u3009 ~ 0.26 kpc for a galaxy with a stellar mass of ~10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. MACS0647-JD is likely somewhat less massive (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-2\">7.2</a>). Scatter in galaxy sizes is large: ~0.3\u00a0dex, or a factor of ~2, as found for well-studied samples at 3 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 5 (Ferguson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r57\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r57\">2004</a>). So our derived <em>r</em><sub>1/2</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.1\u00a0kpc is on the small side, though not beyond expectations (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f16\">16</a>). Furthermore, we may only be detecting a bright star-forming knot in a larger galaxy. These knots typically have sizes of ~0.1\u00a0kpc as observed in high-redshift (5 &lt; <em>z</em> &lt; 8) lensed galaxies (Franx et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r62\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r62\">1997</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r23\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r23\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r24\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r24\">2012a</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f16\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f16\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f16_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 16.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 16.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f16_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 16.</strong>\u00a0Upper limit on MACS0647-JD half-light radius (deconvolved and delensed) compared to observed mean galaxy sizes from Oesch et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r120\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r120\">2010a</a>) and Mosleh et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r117\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r117\">2012</a>) extrapolated to <em>z</em> ~ 11. The intrinsic scatter in galaxy sizes is a factor of ~2 as measured for well-studied samples at <em>z</em> ~ 4 (Ferguson et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r57\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r57\">2004</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f16\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f16\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f16\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f16\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s8\" name=\"apj451260s8\">8.\u00a0NUMBER COUNT AND STAR FORMATION RATE DENSITIES AT <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Our discovery of a 26th magnitude <em>z</em> \u2248 11 candidate in 17 cluster lensing fields (~78 arcmin<sup>2</sup>) agrees with rough expectations given observed luminosity functions (LFs) at lower redshifts extrapolated to higher redshift and propagated through our lens models.</p><p>The LF at <em>z</em> ~ 8 has recently been robustly constrained at both faint (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r17\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r17\">2011b</a>) and bright (Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>) magnitudes (see also Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r123\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r123\">2012b</a>). Based on the combined <em>HST</em> data from the HUDF09, WFC3 Early Release Science Program (ERS), CANDELS, and BoRG pure parallel fields, Bradley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>) find that the <em>z</em> ~ 8 LF follows a Schechter (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r145\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r145\">1976</a>) function with a normalization <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" />* = 4.3<sup>+3.5</sup><sub>\u22122.1</sub> <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22124</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup>, characteristic rest-frame UV absolute magnitude <em>M</em>*<sub>UV</sub> = \u221220.26<sup>+0.29</sup><sub>\u22120.34</sub>, and faint end slope \u03b1 = \u22121.98<sup>+0.23</sup><sub>\u22120.22</sub>. These data and LF with uncertainties are plotted in blue in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f17\">17</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f17\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f17\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f17_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 17.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 17.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f17_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 17.</strong>\u00a0Observed number counts for both lensed (squares) and field (circles/arrows) galaxies at <em>z</em> \u2248 8, 9.6, and 10.8 as a function of observed F160W AB magnitude. CLASH discoveries of candidates at <em>z</em> \u2248 10.8 (MACS0647-JD; this work) and <em>z</em> \u2248 9.6 (MACS1149-JD; Zheng et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>) in ~78 arcmin<sup>2</sup> (17 cluster lensing fields) are plotted as the red and yellow squares, respectively, in units of counts per unit magnitude, redshift, and square arcminute. The blue square indicates the single robust <em>z</em> \u2248 7.5 candidate (A1689-zD1; Bradley et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r23\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r23\">2008</a>) identified in 11 non-CLASH cluster fields covering 21 arcmin<sup>2</sup> (Bouwens et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r13\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r13\">2009</a>). Cyan and orange circles represent counts in unlensed fields at <em>z</em> ~ 8 and 10.3, respectively, from the BoRG survey (Bradley et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>) as well as CANDELS, ERS, and HUDF09 (Oesch et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>) including UDFj-39546284 (Bouwens et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>). The arrows give both 1\u03c3 and 90% confidence upper limits from Oesch et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>). The bottom dashed curves are expected counts with uncertainties from the Bradley et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>) <em>z</em> ~ 8 luminosity function and extrapolated to <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 and 10.8 assuming an evolution of <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.30, similar to that found in, e.g., Bouwens et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r15\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r15\">2008</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r18\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r18\">2012a</a>; also see Figure <a href=\"#apj451260f18\">18</a>). This yields F160W <em>m</em>* ~ 27.0, 27.7, and 28.3 at these redshifts, plotted as a small star along each curve. We then simulate the lensing of these expected counts in our CLASH WFC3/IR survey area using mass models of the 17 clusters and excluding the area (~17%) covered by foreground objects. These are given as the upper solid curves, which are consistent with the CLASH discoveries (red and yellow squares).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f17\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f17\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f17\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f17\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We then assumed an evolving LF in which <em>M</em>* varies linearly with redshift while <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/phgr.gif\" alt=\"phgr\" align=\"absmiddle\" />* and \u03b1 are fixed. Previous work (e.g., Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r15\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r15\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>) has shown that <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> ~ 0.3 yields good agreement to data at 4 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 8 (see also Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f18\">18</a>). So we first assumed that this holds out to <em>z</em> ~ 11.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f18\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f18\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f18_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 18.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 18.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f18_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 18.</strong>\u00a0CLASH constraints on the cosmic star formation rate density at <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 and 10.8 compared to previous estimates from 0 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10 as compiled and corrected for dust extinction by Bouwens et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>). We adopt the <em>z</em> ~ 8 LF from Bradley et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>) and assume <em>M</em>* evolves with linearly redshift. MACS0647-JD constrains <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.33<sup>+0.35</sup><sub>\u22120.15</sub>, while MACS1149-JD (Zheng et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>) constrains <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.62<sup>+0.62</sup><sub>\u22120.24</sub>, yielding a joint constraint of <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.44<sup>+0.29</sup><sub>\u22120.15</sub>. We integrate these LFs down to 0.05\u2009<em>L</em>*(<em>z</em> = 3), or <em>M</em><sub>UV, 1400</sub> \u2248 \u221217.7 AB mag, to obtain rest-frame UV luminosity densities (magenta and green points with the gray shaded region giving the joint constraint). The yellow point is an alternative estimate of the <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 SFRD from Zheng et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>) rescaled by 12/17 to account for the larger search volume behind 17 clusters. An extrapolation from <em>z</em> ~ 8 assuming <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.30 is shown as the dashed blue line. Points along the bottom blue curve are observed while the upper orange curve is corrected for extinction by Bouwens et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>). We assume that a star formation rate of one solar mass per year (left axis) produces a UV (0.14\u2009\u03bcm) luminosity of 8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>27</sup> erg s<sup>\u22121</sup> Hz<sup>\u22121</sup> (right axis), as from a Salpeter (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r141\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r141\">1955</a>) IMF truncated between 0.1 and 125\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. A Chabrier (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r35\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r35\">2003</a>) IMF would yield lower SFRD by a factor of ~1.8. Previous data are from Schiminovich et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r147\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r147\">2005</a>), Oesch et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r121\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r121\">2010b</a>), Reddy &amp; Steidel (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r136\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r136\">2009</a>), Bouwens et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r14\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r14\">2007</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>), and Oesch et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>) as described in Bouwens et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>, see their Figure 19).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f18\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f18\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f18\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f18\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We convolved this evolving LF through our lens models for the 17 CLASH clusters studied in this work, accounting for both the brightening of sources and the reduction in search area due to the magnifications. Some of these models have been published (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r178\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r178\">2011a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r176\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r176\">2012b</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r40\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r40\">2012</a>; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>; and this work) and the rest will be detailed in upcoming work. We applied masks to the lensed regions, restricting our search area to the WFC3/IR observations and excluding regions covered by foreground objects (~17% of the total area) according to our SExtractor segmentation maps. Our total search area for 17 clusters is ~78 arcmin<sup>2</sup> (as observed and lensed).</p><p>The resulting expected lensed number counts for <em>z</em> ~ 8, 9.6, and 10.8 are plotted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f17\">17</a>. These are consistent with CLASH observed ~26th magnitude number counts at <em>z</em> ~ 10.8 (this work) as well as <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 (MACS1149-JD; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>).</p><p>However, the observed <em>z</em> ~ 10 number counts in the field are a factor of ~4 lower than expected based on an evolving LF such as this, suggesting a sharp drop-off in star formation density at these redshifts (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>). To test for such a drop-off, we allowed for more (or less) rapid evolution in <em>M</em>* at <em>z</em> &gt; 8, still as extrapolated from the Bradley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>) LF at <em>z</em> ~ 8. We found that MACS0647-JD constrains <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.33<sup>+0.35</sup><sub>\u22120.15</sub>, and MACS1149-JD constrains <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.62<sup>+0.62</sup><sub>\u22120.24</sub>, yielding a joint constraint of <em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> = 0.44<sup>+0.29</sup><sub>\u22120.15</sub>.</p><p>We then integrated these LFs (with uncertainties) down to 0.05\u2009<em>L</em>*<sub><em>z</em> = 3</sub> (<em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> = \u221217.7) to obtain SFRDs that can be compared directly to previously published estimates (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f18\">18</a>). We found SFRD =(1.4<sup>+1.3</sup><sub>\u22121.1</sub>) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22123</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> at <em>z</em> ~ 11 and (1.1<sup>+1.0</sup><sub>\u22120.9</sub>) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22123</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> at <em>z</em> ~ 9.6. For consistency with the other measurements derived and compiled by Bouwens et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r19\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r19\">2012b</a>), we assumed that an SFR of one solar mass per year produces a UV (0.14\u2009\u03bcm) luminosity of 8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>27</sup>\u2009erg\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Hz<sup>\u22121</sup>, as from a Salpeter (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r141\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r141\">1955</a>) IMF truncated between 0.1 and 125\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>.</p><p>Our SFRD estimate at <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 is consistent with an independent estimate of (1.8<sup>+4.3</sup><sub>\u22121.1</sub>) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22123</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> based on MACS1149-JD presented in Zheng et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>). Only 12 clusters were searched in that work. To account for the larger volume now searched without additional ~26th magnitude <em>z</em> ~ 10 candidates, we rescaled this estimate by a factor of 12/17, yielding (1.3<sup>+3.0</sup><sub>\u22120.8</sub>) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22123</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup>. This value is plotted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f18\">18</a>.</p><p>Our joint constraint from both galaxies on SFRD at <em>z</em> &gt; 8 is also plotted as the gray shaded region in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f18\">18</a>. Given the large uncertainties, we cannot confidently discriminate between the trend observed at lower redshifts (<em>dM</em>*/<em>dz</em> ~ 0.30) and the sharp drop-off suggested by the paucity of <em>z</em> ~ 10 galaxies detected in the field.</p><p>The dominant uncertainty in our SFRD measurement is the Poisson uncertainty of ~0.7 dex (a factor of ~5) given our single detection. Subdominant uncertainties include cosmic variance in 17 independent fields (e.g., Trenti &amp; Stiavelli <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r162\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r162\">2008</a>), uncertainties in the lens models (e.g., Brada\u010d et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r21\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r21\">2009</a>), and incompleteness in our ability to detect ~26th magnitude galaxies (25.4 &lt; F160W &lt; 26.4) after masking out areas corresponding to foreground galaxies. Accounting for overlapping regions in the source plane (multiple images) would slightly increase our derived SFRD.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s9\" name=\"apj451260s9\">9.\u00a0CONCLUSIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have discovered a candidate for the earliest galaxy yet known at <em>z</em> = 10.7<sup>+0.6</sup><sub>\u22120.4</sub> (95% CLs) when the universe was 427<sup>\u221230</sup><sub>+21</sub> Myr old. The galaxy is strongly lensed by MACSJ0647.7+7015 producing three magnified images, including two observed at ~26th magnitude AB (~162 and 136 nJy) in <em>HST</em> WFC3/IR F160W imaging (~1.4\u20131.7\u2009\u03bcm). The intrinsic (delensed) magnitude is ~20 nJy (~28.2\u2009mag) based on our lens models for a galaxy at <em>z</em> ~ 11. The unattenuated continuum is ~0.25\u00a0mag brighter (lensed ~0.2 \u03bcJy in the brightest image).</p><p><em>Spitzer</em>/IRAC upper limits further support the high-redshift solution. We tested a broad range of lower redshift interlopers, including some previously published as high-redshift candidates, and showed that none is able to reproduce the observed <em>HST</em>+<em>Spitzer</em> photometry. Galaxies of known types at <em>z</em> &lt; 9.5 are formally ruled out at 7.2\u03c3, with the next most likely alternative being an early type and/or dusty galaxy at <em>z</em> ~ 2.5. Our Bayesian priors assume that a <em>z</em> ~ 2 galaxy is over 80\u00a0times more likely than a <em>z</em> ~ 11 galaxy, making our <em>z</em> ~ 11 claim more conservative than if such a prior were neglected. For MACS0647-JD to be at <em>z</em> &lt; 9.5, it appears that it would have to belong to a new class of objects not yet observed.</p><p>The discoveries of both MACS0647-JD at <em>z</em> ~ 10.7 (this work) and MACS1149-JD at <em>z</em> ~ 9.6 (Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>) in CLASH observations of 17 clusters to date are consistent with extrapolations of luminosity functions observed at lower redshifts (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r18\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r18\">2012a</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r25\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r25\">2012b</a>), assuming a linear evolution of <em>M</em>* with redshift, and as convolved through our lens models. If these extrapolations are valid to <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10, then low-luminosity galaxies could have reionized the universe (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r18\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r18\">2012a</a>; Kuhlen &amp; Faucher-Gigu\u00e8re <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r100\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r100\">2012</a>). However, these extrapolations are in conflict with the paucity of <em>z</em> ~ 10 galaxies discovered in unlensed fields, suggesting a rapid buildup in star formation density between <em>z</em> ~ 10 and 8 (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r122\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r122\">2012a</a>). Our data do not allow us to discriminate between these two scenarios, given the large uncertainties dominated by the Poisson statistics of these two detections.</p><p>MACS0647-JD is likely close to the characteristic luminosity for a <em>z</em> ~ 11 galaxy (~1\u20133 <em>L</em>*), producing a few\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>, with an inferred stellar mass of roughly ~10<sup>8</sup>\u201310<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, and a half-light radius of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 100\u00a0pc (deconvolved and delensed). This is smaller by a factor of a few than the average size expected as extrapolated from lower redshifts (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r120\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r120\">2010a</a>; Mosleh et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r117\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r117\">2012</a>) with an intrinsic scatter in sizes of perhaps a factor of ~2 (Ferguson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r57\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r57\">2004</a>). The size of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 100\u00a0pc is similar to the sizes of bright knots observed in lensed galaxies at 5 &lt; <em>z</em> &lt; 8 (Franx et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r62\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r62\">1997</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r23\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r23\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r24\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r24\">2012a</a>).</p><p>Thanks to the magnified views afforded us by gravitational lensing, this galaxy may be studied further with existing and future large telescopes, including the <em>James Webb Space Telescope</em> (<em>JWST</em>; Gardner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r65\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r65\">2006</a>; Stiavelli <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r158\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r158\">2009</a>) and extremely large ground-based telescopes constructed in the northern hemisphere. Unfortunately due to its high declination of +70, it is not accessible to southern telescopes such as the Atacama Large Millimeter Array (ALMA).</p><p>This <em>z</em> ~ 11 candidate approaches the redshift limit of galaxies detectable by <em>Hubble</em>'s WFC3/IR camera. Galaxies at <em>z</em> &gt; 12 would drop out completely of the F140W filter and to an increasing degree in F160W until <em>z</em> ~ 13, when all the light redward of Lyman-\u03b1 would be redshifted beyond the observable wavelength range.</p></div><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank Nor Pirzkal for useful discussions regarding the WFC3/IR backgrounds and contributing the single emission line galaxy scenario. We thank the referee for useful comments which helped us improve the manuscript.</p><p>The CLASH Multi-Cycle Treasury Program is based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>. The Space Telescope Science Institute is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc. under NASA contract NAS 5-26555. ACS was developed under NASA contract NAS 5-32864.</p><p>This work is also based in part on observations made with the <em>Spitzer Space Telescope</em>, which is operated by the Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology under a contract with NASA.</p><p>A.Z. is supported by contract research Internationale Spitzenforschung II-1 of the Baden-W\u00fcrttemberg Stiftung. We wish to acknowledge the support of the Michigan State University High Performance Computing Center and the Institute for Cyber-Enabled Research. S.S. was supported by the DFG cluster of excellence Origin and Structure of the Universe. The work of L.A.M. was carried out at Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology, under a contract with NASA. The Dark Cosmology Centre is funded by the DNRF.</p><p><em>Facilities:</em> <em>HST</em> (WFC3, ACS) - Hubble Space Telescope satellite, <em>Spitzer</em> (IRAC) - Spitzer Space Telescope satellite</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=11%2F10&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+distances+and+redshifts\">galaxies: distances and redshifts</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=11%2F13&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+evolution\">galaxies: evolution</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=11%2F18&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+high-redshift\">galaxies: high-redshift</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=2%2F46&amp;code_desc=gravitational+lensing%3A+strong\">gravitational lensing: strong</a></p>\n\t</div>\n<!-- Start Reprint Link -->\n\t<!-- End Reprint Link --><!-- Start Journal RSS feed -->\n                    <div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper jnl-notifications-wrapper--rss\">\n                        <a Journal RSS feed -->\n\t\t\t\t\t<!-- Start Email Alert -->\n    <!-- Start Email Alert -->\n        <div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n            <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert
