rowspan=\"1\">[10.20\u201311.47]</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p><small>\n<strong>Notes.</strong> BPZ results assuming that MACS0647-JD is well modeled by our SED templates (Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5\">5</a>). All likelihoods include a Bayesian prior, which assumes that galaxies of this (unlensed) magnitude are over 80\u00a0times more likely to be at <em>z</em> ~ 2 than at <em>z</em> ~ 11. See also Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f8\">8</a>.\n<a name=\"apj451260t5fn1\" id=\"apj451260t5fn1\"></a><sup> a</sup>Approximate AB magnitudes are given in parentheses. (See also Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t3\">3</a>.) Note JD3 is significantly fainter.\n<a name=\"apj451260t5fn2\" id=\"apj451260t5fn2\"></a><sup> b</sup>Joint likelihood of multiple images weighted equally.\n<a name=\"apj451260t5fn3\" id=\"apj451260t5fn3\"></a><sup> c</sup>Likelihood based on integrated photometry of multiple images.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t5_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/762/1/32\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/suppdata/apj451260t5_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>In Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t4\">4</a>, we also provide joint likelihoods based on the brighter two images and all three images equally weighted. To properly downweight the fainter image, we also analyzed the integrated photometry of all three images (with uncertainties added in quadrature). Based on this analysis including our Bayesian prior, and assuming MACS0647-JD is a galaxy well described by our template set (see also Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-1\">5.1</a>), we found <em>z</em> = 10.7<sup>+0.6</sup><sub>\u22120.4</sub> (95% CL) with a ~3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup> likelihood that MACS0647-JD is at <em>z</em> &lt; 9. This likelihood corresponds to a 7.2\u03c3 confidence that MACS0647-JD is at <em>z</em> &gt; 9. The joint likelihood analysis (weighting all images equally) yields a similar 95% CL [10.2\u201311.1] and a more conservative <em>P</em>(<em>z</em> &lt; 9) ~2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u22128</sup> or <em>z</em> &gt; 9 at 5.5\u03c3.</p><p>The strong confidence in the high-redshift solution requires the combined <em>HST</em> and <em>Spitzer</em> photometry. Without the <em>Spitzer</em> photometry, the <em>z</em> &gt; 9 likelihood would drop to 95% for the summed <em>HST</em> photometry. Similarly, we would find <em>P</em>(<em>z</em> &gt; 9) ~91% for JD1 individually. However, the most likely solutions for JD2 and JD3 would be early types at <em>z</em> ~ 4. We would expect such galaxies to be ~23\u2009mag in the <em>Spitzer</em> observations, which is extremely unlikely (as quantified above) given the measured photometry (see also Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s3-2\">3.2</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s4-2\" name=\"apj451260s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0LePHARE</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We also used LePHARE (Arnouts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r4\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r4\">1999</a>; Ilbert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r79\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r79\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r80\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r80\">2009</a>) to independently estimate the photometric redshifts. For this analysis, we used an SED template library primarily from Ilbert et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r80\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r80\">2009</a>) as optimized for the COSMOS survey (Scoville et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r150\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r150\">2007a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r151\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r151\">2007b</a>; Koekemoer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r97\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r97\">2007</a>). This includes three ellipticals and seven spirals as generated by Polletta et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r130\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r130\">2007</a>) using the GRASIL code (Silva et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r155\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r155\">1998</a>), as well as 12 starburst galaxies with ages ranging from 30\u00a0Myr to 3\u00a0Gyr generated by GALAXEV based on Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r29\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r29\">2003</a>). We supplemented these with four additional elliptical templates for a total of seven ellipticals.</p><p>We added dust extinction in 10 steps up to <em>E</em>(<em>B</em> \u2212 <em>V</em>) = 0.6. (Stronger degrees of extinction are explored in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-1-1\">5.1.1</a>.) Four different dust laws were explored: Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r32\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r32\">2000</a>); Calzetti plus two variations on a 2170\u2009\u00c5 bump; and Prevot et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r133\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r133\">1984</a>) as observed for the SMC.</p><p>We adopted the Ben\u00edtez (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r9\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r9\">2000</a>) prior as implemented in LePHARE. The results were consistent with those from BPZ: <em>z</em> = 10.6<sup>+0.6</sup><sub>\u22120.2</sub> (JD1), <em>z</em> = 10.6<sup>+0.5</sup><sub>\u22120.3</sub> (JD2), and <em>z</em> = 10.1<sup>+0.3</sup><sub>\u22120.3</sub> (JD3), each at 68% CL. A secondary solution of <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 was reported for JD3 with a peak likelihood 10\u00a0times less than that of the best-fit high-redshift solution.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s5\" name=\"apj451260s5\">5.\u00a0LOWER REDSHIFT INTERLOPERS RULED OUT</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this section, we consider a broad range of <em>z</em> &lt; 11 possibilities. As found in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a>, the <em>z</em> &lt; 9 likelihood is formally ~3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup> assuming MACS0647-JD is a galaxy well modeled by our SED templates. Though strongly disfavored, a <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 early type and/or dusty galaxy is the most likely alternative, as we discuss further in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-1\">5.1</a>. We reanalyzed previously published <em>J</em>-dropouts and found them most likely to be at intermediate redshift (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-2\">5.2</a>). Objects within the Galaxy are less likely, as this would require three objects with extremely rare colors (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f13\">13</a>) at positions consistent with strongly lensed multiple images according to our lens models (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6\">6</a>). Nevertheless, we found that the only stars or brown dwarfs consistent with the observed colors are rare, transient post-asymptotic giant branch (AGB) flare-ups, though these would be far more luminous if observed within the Galaxy (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-3\">5.3</a>). Solar system objects would have likely exhibited parallax motion and are inconsistent with the observed colors (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-4\">5.4</a>). Intermediate-redshift long-duration multiply imaged supernovae (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-5\">5.5</a>) and emission line galaxies (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-6\">5.6</a>) are also extremely unlikely. We conclude that MACS0647-JD is most likely either at <em>z</em> ~ 11 or exhibits unique photometry yet to be observed in any other known object.</p><h3 id=\"apj451260s5-1\" name=\"apj451260s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Intermediate Redshift Galaxy?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><h4 id=\"apj451260s5-1-1\" name=\"apj451260s5-1-1\">5.1.1.\u00a0SED Constraints</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>While we found <em>P</em>(<em>z</em> &lt; 9) ~ 3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221213</sup>, the next best alternative to <em>z</em> ~ 11 is an early-type galaxy (ETG) at <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f9\">9</a>). At <em>z</em> ~ 2.65, the 4000\u2009\u00c5 break is redshifted to 1.46\u00a0\u03bcm, coinciding with Lyman\u03b1 (1216\u2009\u00c5) redshifted to <em>z</em> ~ 11.0. However, 4000\u2009\u00c5 breaks are not expected to be as strong as observed for MACS0647-JD (Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f10\">10</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f11\">11</a>). JD1 features a <em>J</em><sub>125</sub> \u2212 <em>H</em><sub>160</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 3 magnitude break between F125W and F160W as well as a ~1\u2009mag break between F140W and F160W. Thus, low-redshift ETGs yield a significantly worse SED fit than <em>z</em> ~ 11 for all three images as quantified in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f8\">8</a> and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t5\">5</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0Observed NIR photometry from <em>HST</em> WFC3/IR and <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC (filled circles and triangles) compared to the expected fluxes (open squares) from two SEDs: the <em>z</em> = 11.0 starburst from Figure <a href=\"#apj451260f3\">3</a> (blue) and a <em>z</em> = 2.5 early-type galaxy (red). Note that the JD3 plot is scaled differently along the <em>y</em>-axis.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0Observed WFC3/IR colors (shaded 68% confidence regions) for JD1 (solid lines), JD2 (dashed lines), and JD3 (dotted lines) plotted against those predicted with the BPZ template set from young starburst (blue) to early type (yellow\u2013orange\u2013red) as a function of redshift. The three panels plot flux ratios in F125W/F160W (left), F125W/F140W (middle), and F140W/F160W (right). The corresponding colors in magnitudes are given along the right axes.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f11\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f11\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f11_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 11.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 11.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f11_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 11.</strong>\u00a0Observed colors in WFC3/IR F125W \u2212 F160W and F160W \u2212 IRAC ch1 plotted as black lines (95% confidence limits) versus those predicted from the current BPZ template library (lines colored as a function of redshift and made thicker for earlier galaxy types).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f11\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f11\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>To explore an even broader range of galaxy SED models than used in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a>, we utilized the flexible stellar population synthesis (FSPS) models from Conroy et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r42\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r42\">2009</a>) and Conroy &amp; Gunn (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r41\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r41\">2010</a>). They provide simple stellar population (SSP) models that span ages of 5.5 &#x2264; log(age/yr) &#x2264;10.175 and metallicities of 0.0002 &#x2264; <em>Z</em> &#x2264; 0.03 (where <em>Z</em><sub>\u2609</sub> = 0.019). Nebular emission lines are not included. We convolved their SSP models with SFHs ranging from the single early burst (SSP) to exponentially declining (\"\u03c4 models\"), continuous (constant rate), and exponentially rising (\"inverted \u03c4 models\"). The latter rising SFH likely describes high-redshift galaxies best according to both observations (Maraston et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r110\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r110\">2010</a>; Papovich et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r126\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r126\">2011</a>; Reddy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r135\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r135\">2012</a>) and simulations (Finlator et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r59\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r59\">2011</a>). Finally, we added a variable degree (up to <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> = 30\u2009mag) of Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r32\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r32\">2000</a>) dust extinction with <em>R<sub>V</sub></em> = <em>A<sub>V</sub></em>/<em>E</em><sub><em>B</em> \u2212 <em>V</em></sub> = 4.05.</p><p>To uncover the most likely solutions in different regions of this multidimensional parameter space, we began with relatively coarse grid searches with redshift intervals of 0.1 and ~9 steps in each of the four other free parameters. We then zoomed in on the higher likelihood regions, found again to be roughly <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 and 11. Finally, we ran Powell (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r132\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r132\">1964</a>) minimizations to find the best-fitting SEDs at each of these redshifts.</p><p>We supplemented these SEDs with a suite of smooth \u03c4 models with stochastic bursts superposed (e.g., Kauffmann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r87\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r87\">2003</a>; Salim et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r140\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r140\">2007</a>), as well as truncated (\"quenched\") SFHs designed to reproduce the colors of post-starburst (K+A) galaxies.</p><p>Our results with this combined template set confirm that a <em>z</em> ~ 11 model fits MACS0647-JD best, while evolved and/or dusty galaxies at <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 provide the best alternatives but are still significantly worse statistically. The best-fitting intermediate-redshift template to the summed photometry (<em>z</em> ~ 2.7; ~400\u00a0Myr old; <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> ~ 0.8\u2009mag) with \u03c7<sup>2</sup> = 57.6 is only ~10<sup>\u22129</sup> times as likely as the best-fitting <em>z</em> ~ 11 template (<em>z</em> ~ 10.9; ~6\u00a0Myr old; <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> = 0) yielding \u03c7<sup>2</sup> = 16.9 with <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 14 degrees of freedom given the 19 photometric measurements and <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 5 free parameters (see discussion in Andrae et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r2\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r2\">2010</a>).</p><p>The uncertainties on the <em>z</em> ~ 11 SED parameters are quantified in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-3\">7.3</a>. A proper calculation of the redshift likelihoods based on these templates would require an estimate of the prior likelihoods in this multidimensional parameter space, which is beyond the scope of this work. And while these templates probe a broad parameter space, we derive our primary photometric redshift estimates in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s4\">4</a> from templates that have been well calibrated to match the observed photometry of galaxies with spectroscopic redshifts.</p><p>We note that there is no evidence that <em>z</em> &gt; 2 ETGs have significantly different SEDs than our ETG models calibrated at lower redshifts. The highest redshift ETG observed to date is HUDF-1446 with a spectroscopic redshift of <em>z</em> = 2.67 (Damjanov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r46\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r46\">2011</a>). Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r38\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r38\">2006</a>) published a photometric redshift of <em>z</em> = 2.74 \u00b1 0.44 for this object using BPZ, in good agreement with the true redshift. Their ETG templates yielded a good fit to the ACS (<em>B</em><sub>435</sub><em>V</em><sub>606</sub><em>i</em>'<sub>775</sub><em>z</em>'<sub>850</sub>) and NICMOS (<em>J</em><sub>110</sub><em>H</em><sub>160</sub>) photometry, including the <em>J</em><sub>110</sub> \u2212 <em>H</em><sub>160</sub> = 1.89 \u00b1 0.13 break with <em>H</em><sub>160</sub> = 23.074 \u00b1 0.098 and significant detections in all ACS filters.</p></div><h4 id=\"apj451260s5-1-2\" name=\"apj451260s5-1-2\">5.1.2.\u00a0Lower Stellar Mass than Observed <em>z</em> &gt; 2 ETGs</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>If MACS0647-JD were at <em>z</em> ~ 2.5 (despite the low likelihood of this from SED fitting) it would likely be the least massive early-type host galaxy observed to date at <em>z</em> &gt; 1. Spectroscopically confirmed <em>z</em> &gt; 1.4 ETGs to date have stellar masses &gt;2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (Damjanov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r46\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r46\">2011</a>). HUDF-1446 at <em>z</em> = 2.67, for example, is ~8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>.</p><p>Our subset of lens models that allow for MACS0647-JD to be at <em>z</em> ~ 3 (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6-3\">6.3</a>) suggest that the magnification of the brightest two images would be \u03bc &gt; 30. Thus, it would be intrinsically ~300\u00a0times fainter than HUDF-1446, with a correspondingly lower stellar mass on the order of ~2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>8</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (and still &lt;10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> if we assume a more conservative magnification factor of \u03bc ~ 10; see also <em>z</em> ~ 11 mass estimates in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s7-2\">7.2</a>).</p><p>Quiescent galaxies of such low masses at <em>z</em> &gt; 2 would be a surprising discovery. Observations to date demonstrate (e.g., Peng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r127\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r127\">2010</a>) that star formation is only significantly quenched by feedback in more massive galaxies, or alternatively as a galaxy is harassed as a satellite of a larger halo. MACS0647-JD is not observed to be a satellite of a galaxy group.</p></div></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s5-2\" name=\"apj451260s5-2\">5.2.\u00a0Comparisons to Previously Published <em>J</em>-dropouts</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The previous highest redshift candidate, UDFj-39546284 (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a>), was detected at 5.8\u03c3 in a single <em>HST</em> band (WFC3/IR F160W) dropping out of F125W and bluer filters also with non-detections in <em>Spitzer</em> yielding a photometric redshift of <em>z</em> = 10.3 \u00b1 0.8. The ultimate inclusion of the F140W filter on WFC3 (Brown &amp; Baggett <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r28\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r28\">2006</a>) and in the CLASH observing program enables us to securely identify MACS0647-JD as the highest redshift galaxy candidate to date. At <em>z</em> ~ 11.0, Lyman\u03b1 is redshifted to ~1.46\u2009\u03bcm, causing the galaxy light to drop out of ~2/3 of the F140W bandpass as well as ~1/5 of F160W. The ratio between these two filling factors (0.8/0.33 ~ 2.4, corresponding to ~1.0\u2009mag) places tight, model-independent constraints on the wavelength of the (redshifted) Lyman break and thus the redshift of MACS0647-JD (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f10\">10</a>). The five NIR <em>HST</em> filters used by CLASH also enabled Zheng et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r174\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r174\">2012</a>) to discover a <em>J</em>-dropout lensed by MACSJ1149.6+2233 and robustly measure its photometric redshift to be <em>z</em> = 9.6 \u00b1 0.2 (68% CL).</p><p>Laporte et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r104\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r104\">2011</a>) identified a <em>J</em>-dropout lensed by Abell 2667 based on VLT (FORS2 and HAWK-I), ACS/F850LP, and <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC (ch1\u2013ch4) photometry. Hayes et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>) then measured a spectroscopic redshift of <em>z</em> = 2.082 for that galaxy, A2667-J1. Laporte et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r104\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r104\">2011</a>) had already emphasized that <em>z</em> &gt; 9 possibilities were excluded based on the significant (6.0\u03c3) ACS detection. We concur with this conclusion after reanalyzing their photometry as provided in Hayes et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>). Only by excluding the ACS data point and assuming no Bayesian redshift prior do <em>z</em> &gt; 9 solutions have significant probability (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f12\">12</a>). If <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC ch3 and ch4 were not available (as is the case with MACS0647-JD) <em>in addition</em> to the ACS detection being unavailable, then the <em>z</em> &gt; 9 likelihood would rise further, yet still be insignificant once the prior is included. The <em>z</em> &gt; 9 likelihood is enhanced further, but only modestly, if the IRAC ch1 and ch2 uncertainties are inflated to yield only 3\u03c3 detections (as is the case for our JD2 IRAC ch2). In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f12\">12</a>, we compare the observed NIR photometry of A2667-J1 and MACS0647-JD1. Our multiband <em>HST</em> photometry of the latter yields significantly tighter upper limits on the non-detections and adds a key data point at 1.4\u2009\u03bcm, resulting in a far greater <em>z</em> ~ 11 likelihood even when accounting for the Bayesian prior which disfavors them (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f8\">8</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f12\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f12\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f12_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 12.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 12.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f12_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 12.</strong>\u00a0Left:comparison of near-infrared photometry of lensed <em>J</em>-dropouts MACS0647-JD1 (this work) and A2667-J1 (Laporte et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r104\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r104\">2011</a>; Hayes et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>). Also overplotted are three SED fits from Figure 2 of Hayes et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>) to the photometry of A2667-J1 at its spectroscopic redshift <em>z</em> = 2.082. The Hayes et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>) photometry plotted here is all from VLT (FORS2 and HAWK-I) except for the 6.0\u03c3 detection in <em>HST</em>/ACS F850LP, the upper diamond with a darker border at 0.91\u2009\u03bcm. Right: photometric redshift probability distribution for A2667-J1 based on our reanalysis of the photometry provided in Hayes et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r75\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r75\">2012</a>) with and without a Bayesian prior. The top panel uses all the available photometry. The middle panel omits the 6.0\u03c3 detection in ACS/F850LP. The bottom panel omits both ACS and <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC ch3 and ch4. The spectroscopic redshift <em>z</em> = 2.082 is indicated by the red vertical lines.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f12\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f12\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We also applied our analysis methods to the photometry of other <em>J</em>-dropouts in the literature. Schaerer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r144\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r144\">2007</a>) showed A1835-#17 was fit well by a dusty (<em>A<sub>V</sub></em> ~ 3.6\u2009mag) starburst at <em>z</em> ~ 0.8. Dickinson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r49\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r49\">2000</a>) presented both <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 2 and <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10 solutions for HDF-N J123656.3+621322. And HUDF-JD2 (Mobasher et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r115\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r115\">2005</a>) has since been shown likely to be a <em>z</em> ~ 1.7 luminous infrared galaxy (Chary et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r36\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r36\">2007</a>). For all three of these <em>J</em>-dropouts, our analysis yields low redshift (2 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 4 or very dusty <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 1) solutions which are strongly preferred given our Bayesian prior.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s5-3\" name=\"apj451260s5-3\">5.3.\u00a0Stars Or Brown Dwarfs?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>MACS0647-JD1, JD2, and JD3 are most likely multiple images of a strongly lensed background galaxy, well behind the <em>z</em> = 0.591 cluster. Their observed colors are extremely rare in our multiband <em>HST</em> catalogs of 17 clusters observed to date (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f13\">13</a>). And they lie at or near the predicted positions of multiply lensed images (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6\">6</a>). It would be highly unlikely to find three foreground (unlensed) objects with such rare colors coincidentally at these positions. Still we consider here possible interlopers within the Galaxy, namely stars, brown dwarfs, and (in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s5-4\">5.4</a>) solar system objects including Kuiper Belt objects and Oort cloud objects.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f13\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f13\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f13_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 13.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 13.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f13_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 13.</strong>\u00a0Observed NIR colors of the <em>J</em>-dropouts (red diamonds with 1\u03c3 uncertainties) plotted against those observed for all other 20,746 CLASH sources brighter than 28th magnitude in both F160W and F140W and also observed in F125W (filled circles and density map). The horizontal axis gives the ratio of the F160W flux to the maximum flux in all bluer WFC3/IR filters. The vertical axis gives a similar flux ratio but for F140W. Three objects with colors similar to the <em>J</em>-dropouts appear to be spurious IR artifacts based on visual inspection, and we mark these with X's.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f13\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f13\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f13\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f13\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>JD1 and JD2 are perhaps marginally resolved with deconvolved FWHM <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 (0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3 observed with a 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 PSF). We performed two independent analyses attempting to determine whether the observed FWHM was large enough to definitively distinguish it from the stellar locus. These analyses reached different conclusions. Therefore, we turn to other lines of evidence to rule out stars and smaller objects.</p><p>Stars are relatively plentiful in this field as the Galactic latitude is relatively low (+25<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />1). We used the online tool TRILEGAL<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj451260fn1\">21</a></sup> (Girardi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r68\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r68\">2005</a>) to calculate that we may expect ~5 late-type M dwarfs of ~26th magnitude or fainter within our FOV. However, the predicted colors are <em>J</em><sub>125</sub> \u2212 <em>H</em><sub>160</sub> ~ 0.4, a break significantly weaker than that observed.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f14\">14</a>, observed and expected colors of stars and brown dwarfs (including types M, L, T, and Y) are plotted versus those observed for the <em>J</em>-dropouts. No dwarf color is able to reproduce the observed <em>J</em>-dropout colors. According to models, the colors of extremely cold (~200\u00a0K) Y dwarfs come close to matching the red observed <em>HST</em> NIR colors, but these are expected to be significantly brighter in IRAC by up to 10\u2009mag. The coldest dwarfs yet discovered are Y dwarfs including WISEP J1828+2650 at ~300\u00a0K (Cushing et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r44\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r44\">2011</a>; Kirkpatrick et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r91\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r91\">2012</a>) with colors as plotted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f14\">14</a> from ground-based <em>JH</em> and <em>WISE</em> W2 4.6\u2009\u03bcm observations.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj451260f14\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj451260f14\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f14_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 14.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 14.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260f14_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 14.</strong>\u00a0Observed colors in <em>J</em><sub>125</sub>, <em>H</em><sub>160</sub>, and at 4.5\u2009\u03bcm plotted as black lines (95% confidence contours) versus those observed and predicted for stars and brown dwarfs. Colors derived from stellar spectra observed with IRTF (Cushing et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r45\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r45\">2005</a>; Rayner et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r134\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r134\">2009</a>) are plotted as open magenta circles for dwarfs and open black star symbols for giants and supergiants. Blue error bars are observed photometry (ground-based and <em>WISE</em>) for Y dwarfs (Kirkpatrick et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r91\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r91\">2012</a>). Open black diamonds are post-AGB flare-ups with dust ejecta observed with 2MASS and <em>WISE</em>; the upper diamond is \"Sakurai's object\" (Duerbeck &amp; Benetti <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r51\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r51\">1996</a>) and the lower diamond is WISE J1810\u22123305 (Gandhi et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r64\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r64\">2012</a>). Simulated dwarf spectra from Hubeny &amp; Burrows (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r78\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r78\">2007</a>) are plotted as filled circles colored as a function of temperature.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj451260f14\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadFigure/figure/apj451260f14\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj451260f14\" href=\"/0004-637X/762/1/32/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj451260f14\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Of the stellar spectra observed with the Infrared Telescope Facility (IRTF; Cushing et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r45\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r45\">2005</a>; Rayner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r134\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r134\">2009</a>), the M8III red giant WX Piscium (IRAS 01037+1219; Ulrich et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r164\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r164\">1966</a>; Decin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r48\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r48\">2007</a>) comes closest to matching the observed colors of MACS0647-JD. However, such a large, bright star (<em>M</em> ~ \u22124) would need to be well outside the Galaxy (~10 Mpc distant) to be observed at 26th magnitude in F160W (as argued in Dickinson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r49\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r49\">2000</a> and Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r16\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r16\">2011a</a> for two previous <em>z</em> ~ 10 candidates). If MACS0647-JD were within the Galaxy (out to ~10\u00a0kpc), it would have an absolute magnitude of <em>M</em> ~ +11 or fainter, consistent with a red dwarf in terms of magnitude but not color as shown above.</p><p>A few red giants in the post-AGB phase have been observed to flare up apparently as the result of a helium burning \"thermal pulse,\" which triggers the ejection of a dust shell. \"Sakurai's object\" (Duerbeck &amp; Benetti <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r51\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r51\">1996</a>) and WISE J1810\u22123305 (Gandhi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r64\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r64\">2012</a>) do have similar colors to our <em>J</em>-dropouts. But again these are very bright events, observed at 0.34 \u00b1 0.01 and 2.74 \u00b1 0.06 Jy, respectively, in the <em>H</em> band with the Two Micron All Sky Survey (2MASS). Believed to be a few kpc distant, they would need to be removed to several Mpc to be observed at ~0.1 \u03bcJy as our <em>J</em>-dropouts. These are also rare events, lasting on the order of 100\u00a0years (but varying more rapidly), such that only these two have been reported to date. It would be highly unlikely to detect three such events occurring at the same time in the same <em>HST</em> field.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s5-4\" name=\"apj451260s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0Solar System Objects?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>If the <em>J</em>-dropouts were solar system objects, we would expect to have detected their proper motions in our six epochs of F160W/F140W imaging spanning 56\u00a0days (Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f4\">4</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f6\">6</a>). Only an Oort cloud object at ~50,000 AU would be orbiting the Sun sufficiently slowly for us not to have detected its motion. But Oort cloud objects are expected to be significantly fainter (~58th magnitude assuming a diameter of ~20\u00a0km; Sheppard <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r152\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r152\">2010</a>) and have different colors than those observed here. Even a maximally large rocky planet with a diameter of ~200,000\u2009km and 100% albedo would only be ~37th\u2009mag. Larger objects would be brown dwarfs, which we ruled out in Section 5.3 based on their colors. Oort cloud objects are also expected to have colors different than those observed for MACS0647-JD. Benecchi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r8\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r8\">2011</a>) measured <em>HST</em>/NICMOS F110W\u2212F160W colors of 80 trans-Neptunian objects and found that they have <em>HST</em> F110W\u2212F160W colors clustered around ~0.6 with none redder than 0.8. To be observed at ~26th\u2009mag, an Oort cloud object would have to be the size of a small moon. Even if such objects exist, they are almost certainly rare, or they would have been discovered by now. It would be highly improbable to discover the first three within a single <em>HST</em> FOV.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s5-5\" name=\"apj451260s5-5\">5.5.\u00a0Lensed Supernova?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The <em>J</em>-dropouts do not exhibit any significant temporal variations in brightness either over our 56\u00a0days of observations (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f5\">5</a>), ruling out most transient phenomena such as supernovae. However, Type IIP supernovae can plateau to a roughly constant magnitude for ~100\u00a0days (e.g., Arcavi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r3\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r3\">2012</a>) which we would observe to last ~100(1 + <em>z</em>) days due to cosmic time dilation (Blondin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r11\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r11\">2008</a>). A Type Ia supernova at <em>z</em> ~ 4 would be observed to have magnitudes and colors similar to those observed in <em>HST</em> for MACS0647-JD. A Type IIP plateau supernova would likely be bluer, but could perhaps match the observed <em>HST</em> colors. We would expect to detect it as a bright object at longer wavelengths, but the <em>Spitzer</em> images were obtained 1.5\u00a0years earlier, perhaps before the star went supernova.</p><p>This intriguing scenario is ruled out by the gravitational lens time delays due to MACSJ0647.7+7015, which we estimate to be on the order of 1\u201310\u00a0years between JD1 and JD2 and ~50\u00a0years between these and JD3. Our subset of lens models (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6\">6</a>), which allow for <em>z</em> ~ 4, also suggest that the intrinsic fluxes of all three images are roughly consistent with one another (at least to within a magnitude). Thus, the supernova plateau (several magnitudes brighter than the host galaxy) would have to have lasted 50/(1 + <em>z</em>) ~ 10 years for us to observe it simultaneously in all three images. Even if this were somehow possible, we would then expect to have detected the earlier (least time-delayed) images with <em>Spitzer</em>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj451260s5-6\" name=\"apj451260s5-6\">5.6.\u00a0Emission Line Galaxy?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In principle, an active galactic nucleus/starburst galaxy with an undetected continuum and two or more extremely strong nebular emission lines redshifted into F140W and F160W could reproduce the observed <em>HST</em> colors. The only plausible configuration is that H\u03b2 (4861\u2009\u00c5) and [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (4959\u2009\u00c5) are redshifted to within F140W and F160W, while [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (5007\u00a0\u00c5) is redshifted beyond F140W but within F160W. This is possible for the narrow redshift range 2.20 &lt; <em>z</em> &lt; 2.22. At this redshift, F140W and F160W have rest-frame widths of ~1229 and ~889\u2009\u00c5, respectively. In the case of JD, we measure the flux blueward of F140W to be \u22123.4 \u00b1 3.7 nJy. We conservatively adopt &lt;7.4 nJy as the 2\u03c3 upper limit on the continuum flux. Boosting the F140W flux to the observed ~63\u00a0nJy would require emission lines with a combined equivalent width (EW) of &gt;1229 <b>&#x00d7;</b> (63/7.4 \u2212 1) ~ 9525\u2009\u00c5. Similarly, increasing the F160W flux to the observed ~162 nJy would require a combined EW &gt;889 <b>&#x00d7;</b> (162/7.4 \u2212 1) ~ 19114\u2009\u00c5 (~10<sup>\u221215</sup> erg s<sup>\u22121</sup> cm<sup>\u22122</sup>). Thus, in our configuration assuming a continuum flux of 7.4\u00a0nJy:\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj451260fd1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260fd1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray} &&\\rm EW (H\\beta + [{\\rm O\\,\\mathsc{iii}}]_{4959}) \\approx \\rm 9234\\ {\\rm \\AA}, \\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj451260fd2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260fd2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray} &&\\rm EW (H\\beta + [{\\rm O\\,\\mathsc{iii}}]_{4959} + [{\\rm O\\,\\mathsc{iii}}]_{5007}) \\approx \\rm 18573\\ {\\rm \\AA}, \\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj451260fd3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260fd3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray} &&\\rm EW ([{\\rm O\\,\\mathsc{iii}}]_{5007}) \\hspace{3.61371pt} \\approx (2) - (1) \\approx \\rm 9339\\ {\\rm \\AA}, \\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj451260fd4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260fd4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray} &&\\rm EW ([{\\rm O\\,\\mathsc{iii}}]_{4959}) \\hspace{3.61371pt} \\approx (3) / 3 \\approx \\rm 3113\\ {\\rm \\AA}, \\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj451260fd5\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/762/1/32/Full/apj451260fd5.gif\" alt=\"Equation (5)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray} &&\\rm EW (H\\beta) \\hspace{3.61371pt} \\hspace{27.46295pt} = (1) - (4) \\approx \\rm 6121\\ {\\rm \\AA}, \\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where the line ratio in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj451260fd4\">4</a>) is dictated by the relative transition probabilities.</p><p>An [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (5007\u2009\u00c5) line with EW &gt;9000\u2009\u00c5 would be several times greater than the strongest emission lines observed to date, approaching EW ~2000\u2009\u00c5, for [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (5007\u2009\u00c5) and H\u03b1 (6563\u2009\u00c5) (Atek et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r5\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r5\">2011</a>; van der Wel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r165\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r165\">2011</a>; Shim et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r154\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r154\">2011</a>; Fumagalli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r63\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r63\">2012</a>). If we consider instead the 5\u03c3 continuum limit of 18.4 nJy, we would still require EW ~3959\u2009\u00c5 for [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (5007\u2009\u00c5) and EW ~1659\u2009\u00c5 for H\u03b2. The strongest H\u03b2 lines are robustly predicted to have EW <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 800\u2009\u00c5 even for extremely young stellar populations, according to models like Starburst99<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj451260fn2\">22</a></sup> (Leitherer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r106\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r106\">1999</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r105\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r105\">2010</a>). Finally, given such bright lines, one would also expect a significant contribution of [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (3727\u2009\u00c5) in F110W and F125W, which is not observed.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj451260s6\" name=\"apj451260s6\">6.\u00a0GRAVITATIONAL LENS MODELING</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We identified 24 strongly lensed images of nine background galaxies (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6-1\">6.1</a>), used them to derive lens models using three different methods (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6-2\">6.2</a>), and derived results including magnifications in (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj451260s6-3\">6.3</a>). Importantly, our lens models show that MACS0647-JD1, 2, and 3 are observed in relative positions as expected if they are strongly lensed multiple images of the same galaxy at <em>z</em> ~ 11.</p><h3 id=\"apj451260s6-1\" name=\"apj451260s6-1\">6.1.\u00a0Strongly Lensed Multiple Images</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r175\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r175\">2011a</a>) presented a preliminary gravitational lens mass model of the MACSJ0647.7+7015 cluster core based on pre-CLASH <em>HST</em>/ACS F555W+F814W imaging and their identifications of two background galaxies strongly lensed to produce multiple images. Based on CLASH imaging in 15 additional <em>HST</em> filters and additional lens modeling using the Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj451260r177\" id=\"fnref-apj451260r177\">2009</a>) method, we have now identified seven more galaxies that have been multiply imaged, and we have measured robust photometric redshifts for all nine galaxies. This enables us to model the mass distribution (primarily dark matter) and thus lensing properties in greater detail.</p><p>In addition to the three images of MACS0647-JD at <em>z</em> ~ 11, we observe 21 multiple images of eight background galaxies with photometric redshifts ranging from 2 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 6.5 (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj451260t6\">6</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj451260f1\">1</a>). The candidate <em>z</em> ~ 6.5 system is notable in its own right, consisting of two images observed at magnitudes of ~26.3 and 27.3 in the NIR.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj451260t6\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 6.</b>\u00a0Twenty-four Multiple Images of Nine Strongly Lensed Galaxies</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\"
